<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Taoism</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Religious/philosophical tradition of Chinese origin</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about Taoism as a traditional religion from China. For Taoism as a Chinese philosophy, see <a href="/wiki/Taoist_philosophy" title="Taoist philosophy">Taoist philosophy</a>. For Taoism as a new religious movement/concept in Western world, see <a href="/wiki/History_of_Taoism#Taoism_in_the_West" title="History of Taoism">History of Taoism § Taoism in the West</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Taoism" title="Template:Taoism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Taoism" title="Template talk:Taoism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Taoism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Taoism</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/əm/: 'm' in 'rhythm'">əm</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/əm/: 'm' in 'rhythm'">əm</span></span>/</a></span></span>) or <b>Daoism</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/əm/: 'm' in 'rhythm'">əm</span></span>/</a></span></span>) refers to either a school of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical thought</a> (道家; <i>daojia</i>) or to a <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> (道教; <i>daojiao</i>); both share ideas and concepts of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">Chinese</a> origin and emphasize living in harmony with the <i><a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">Tao</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%81%93" title="wikt:道">道</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Dào</span></i>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'Way', 'Thoroughfare'). The <i><a href="/wiki/Tao_Te_Ching" title="Tao Te Ching">Tao Te Ching</a></i>, a book containing teachings attributed to <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">老子</span>), together with the later <a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">writings of Zhuangzi</a>, are both widely considered the keystone works of Taoism.
</p><p>In Taoism, the <i>Tao</i> is the source of everything and the ultimate principle underlying reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PollardRosenberg2014_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PollardRosenberg2014-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel19822_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel19822-3">[3]</a></sup> Taoism teaches about the various disciplines for achieving perfection through self-cultivation. This can be done through the use of Taoist techniques and by becoming one with the unplanned rhythms of the all, called "the way" or "Tao".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PollardRosenberg2014_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-PollardRosenberg2014-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Taoist ethics vary depending on the particular school, but in general tend to emphasize <i><a href="/wiki/Wu_wei" title="Wu wei">wu wei</a></i> (action without intention), naturalness, simplicity, spontaneity and the <a href="/wiki/Three_Treasures_(Taoism)" title="Three Treasures (Taoism)">Three Treasures</a>: <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">慈</span></span>, compassion, <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">儉</span></span>, frugality and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">不敢爲天下先</span></span>, humility.
</p><p>The roots of Taoism go back at least to the 4th century BCE. Early Taoism drew its cosmological notions from the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Naturalists" title="School of Naturalists">School of Yinyang</a> (Naturalists) and was deeply influenced by one of the oldest texts of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">Chinese culture</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a></i>, which expounds a philosophical system about how to keep human behavior in accordance with the alternating cycles of <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalist</a> <a href="/wiki/Shen_Buhai" title="Shen Buhai">Shen Buhai</a> (c. 400 – c. 337 BCE) may also have been a major influence, expounding a <a href="/wiki/Realpolitik" title="Realpolitik">realpolitik</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Wu_wei" title="Wu wei">wu wei</a></i>, or qualified inaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel198248,_62–63Bishop1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid-E5LZeR7QKwCpgPA92_92]ChingGuisso1991[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidynfrlFZcUG8CpgPA75_75],_119_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel198248,_62–63Bishop1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid-E5LZeR7QKwCpgPA92_92]ChingGuisso1991[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidynfrlFZcUG8CpgPA75_75],_119-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taoism has had a profound influence on Chinese culture in the course of the centuries and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist_priest" title="Taoist priest">Taoists</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">道士</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">dàoshi</span></i>, "masters of the Tao"), a title traditionally attributed only to the clergy and not to their lay followers, usually take care to note the distinction between their ritual tradition and the practices of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">Chinese folk religion</a> and non-Taoist <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ritual_mastery_traditions" title="Chinese ritual mastery traditions">vernacular ritual orders</a>, which are often mistakenly identified as pertaining to Taoism. <a href="/wiki/Chinese_alchemy" title="Chinese alchemy">Chinese alchemy</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Neidan" title="Neidan">neidan</a>), <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astrology" title="Chinese astrology">Chinese astrology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Chan (Zen)</a> <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, several <a href="/wiki/Neijia" title="Neijia">martial arts</a> including <a href="/wiki/Chinese_martial_arts" title="Chinese martial arts">kung fu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feng_shui" title="Feng shui">feng shui</a> and many styles of <a href="/wiki/Qigong" title="Qigong">qigong</a> have been intertwined with Taoism throughout history.
</p><p>Today, the Taoist religion is one of the <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_China" title="Religion in China">five religious doctrines</a> officially recognized by the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">People's Republic of China</a> (PRC), including in its <a href="/wiki/Special_administrative_regions_of_China" title="Special administrative regions of China">special administrative regions</a> (SARs) of <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> It is also a <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Taiwan" title="Religion in Taiwan">major religion</a> in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and has a significant number of adherents in a number of other societies throughout East and Southeast Asia, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Taoism_in_Malaysia" title="Taoism in Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism_in_Singapore" title="Taoism in Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taoism_in_Vietnam" title="Taoism in Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="1585" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png/300px-Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png/450px-Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png/600px-Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Birth places of notable Chinese philosophers from Hundred Schools of Thought in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhou_Dynasty" title="Zhou Dynasty">Zhou Dynasty</a>. Philosophers of Taoism are marked by triangles in dark green.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling_and_pronunciation">Spelling and pronunciation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Spelling and pronunciation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Daoism%E2%80%93Taoism_romanization_issue" title="Daoism–Taoism romanization issue">Daoism–Taoism romanization issue</a></div>
<p>Since the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">Pinyin</a> system for <a href="/wiki/Romanization" title="Romanization">romanizing</a> Mandarin Chinese, there have been those who have felt that "Taoism" would be more appropriately spelled as "Daoism". The <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin Chinese</a> pronunciation for the word <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%81%93" title="wikt:道">道</a></span></span> (way, path) is spelled as <i><a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">tao<sup>4</sup></a></i> in the older <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a> romanization system (from which the spelling 'Taoism' is derived), while it is spelled as <i><a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">dào</a></i> in the newer <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">Pinyin</a> romanization system (from which the spelling "Daoism" is derived). The Wade–Giles <i>tao<sup>4</sup></i> and the Pinyin <i>dào</i> are pronounced identically in Mandarin Chinese (like the unaspirated 't' in 'stop'); despite this, "Taoism" and "Daoism" are often pronounced differently in English vernacular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Categorization">Categorization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Categorization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word <i>Taoism</i> is used to translate different Chinese terms which refer to two semantically distinct fields:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPregadio2008Vol._1,_p._xvi_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPregadio2008Vol._1,_p._xvi-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Taoist religion (<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%81%93%E6%95%8E" title="wikt:道敎">道敎</a></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/D%C3%A0oji%C3%A0o" title="wikt:Dàojiào">Dàojiào</a></span></i>; lit. "teachings of the Tao"), or the "liturgical" aspect<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPregadio2008Vol._1,_p._327,_"Taoshih"_10-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPregadio2008Vol._1,_p._327,_"Taoshih"-10'>[10]</a></sup> – A family of organized religious movements sharing concepts or terminology from "Taoist philosophy";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet1997xxix_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet1997xxix-11">[11]</a></sup> the first of these is recognized as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_Masters" title="Celestial Masters">Celestial Masters</a> school.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Taoist_philosophy" title="Taoist philosophy">Taoist philosophy</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%81%93%E5%AE%B6" title="wikt:道家">道家</a></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/D%C3%A0oji%C4%81" title="wikt:Dàojiā">Dàojiā</a></span></i>; lit. "school or family of the Tao") or "Taology" (<span lang="zh-Hant">道學</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">dàoxué</span></i>; lit. "study of the Tao"), or the mystical aspect<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPregadio2008Vol._1,_p._327,_"Taoshih"_10-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPregadio2008Vol._1,_p._327,_"Taoshih"-10'>[10]</a></sup> – The <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical doctrines</a> based on the texts of the <i><a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Tao_Te_Ching" title="Tao Te Ching">Tao Te Ching</a></i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">道德經</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">dàodéjīng</span></i>) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi</a></i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">莊子</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zhuāngzi</span></i>). The earliest recorded uses of the term Tao to refer to a philosophy or a school of thought are found in the works of classical historians during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn200044_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn200044-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford_Taoism2_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford_Taoism2-13">[13]</a></sup> These works include <a href="/wiki/Zuo_Zhuan" title="Zuo Zhuan">The Commentary of Zhuo</a> (左传;  zuǒ zhuàn) by Zuo Qiuming (左丘明) and in the <a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a> (史記; Shǐjì<i>)</i> by Sima Tan. This usage of the term to narrowly denote a school of thought precedes the emergence of the Celestial Masters and associated later religions. It is unlikely that <a href="/wiki/Zhuang_Zhou" title="Zhuang Zhou">Zhuangzi</a> was familiar with the text of the Tao Te Ching,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford_Taoism_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford_Taoism-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_1989_p._170–171_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graham_1989_p._170–171-15">[15]</a></sup> and Zhuangzi himself may have died before the term was in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_1989_p._170–171_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graham_1989_p._170–171-15">[15]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>In ancient China, the use of the term Taoist to narrowly describe a school of thought, rather than a set of religious teachings, has been recorded as early as 100 BCE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and such usage precedes the emergence of the earliest Taoist religious sects such as the Celestial Masters by at least 300 years.
</p><p>The distinction between Taoist philosophy (道家) and religion (道教) has been maintained by modern pioneers of Chinese philosophy <a href="/wiki/Feng_Youlan" title="Feng Youlan">Feng Youlan</a> (馮友蘭; 1895-1990) and <a href="/wiki/Wing-tsit_Chan" title="Wing-tsit Chan">Wing-tsit Chan</a> (陳榮捷; 1901–1994). The distinction as advocated by outstanding philosophers such as Feng and Chan, however, is rejected by the majority of Western and Japanese scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> It is contested by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermeneutic" title="Hermeneutic">hermeneutic</a> (interpretive) difficulties in the categorization of the different Taoist schools, sects and movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair2001174_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair2001174-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taoism does not fall under an umbrella or a definition of a single <a href="/wiki/Organized_religion" title="Organized religion">organized religion</a> like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abrahamic_traditions" title="Abrahamic traditions">Abrahamic traditions</a>; nor can it be studied as a mere variant of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">Chinese folk religion</a>, as although the two share some similar concepts, much of Chinese folk religion is separate from the tenets and core teachings of Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinet_1997,_p._103_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robinet_1997,_p._103-20">[20]</a></sup> The sinologists Isabelle Robinet and Livia Kohn agree that "Taoism has never been a unified religion, and has constantly consisted of a combination of teachings based on a variety of original revelations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet19972_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet19972-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Chung-ying_Cheng" title="Chung-ying Cheng">Chung-ying Cheng</a> views Taoism as a religion that has been embedded into Chinese history and tradition. "Whether Confucianism, Taoism, or later Chinese Buddhism, they all fall into this pattern of thinking and organizing and in this sense remain religious, even though individually and intellectually they also assume forms of philosophy and practical wisdom."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-22">[22]</a></sup> Chung-ying Cheng also noted that the Taoist view of heaven flows mainly from "observation and meditation, [though] the teaching of the way (<i>Tao</i>) can also include the way of heaven independently of human nature".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-22">[22]</a></sup> In Chinese history, the three religions of Buddhism, Taoism and Confucianism stand on their own independent views, and yet are "involved in a process of attempting to find harmonization and convergence among themselves, so that we can speak of a 'unity of three religious teachings' (<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%89" title="wikt:三">三</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%95%8E" title="wikt:敎">敎</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%90%88" title="wikt:合">合</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%80" title="wikt:一">一</a></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Sānjiào Héyī</span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="The_terms_.22Taoist.22_and_.22Taoism.22_as_a_liturgical_framework"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='The_terms_"Taoist"_and_"Taoism"_as_a_liturgical_framework'>The terms "Taoist" and "Taoism" as a liturgical framework</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title='Edit section: The terms "Taoist" and "Taoism" as a liturgical framework'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Traditionally, the Chinese language does not have terms defining lay people adhering to the doctrines or the practices of Taoism, who fall instead within the field of folk religion. Taoist, in Western <a href="/wiki/Sinology" title="Sinology">sinology</a>, is traditionally used to translate <i><a href="/wiki/Daoshi" title="Daoshi">daoshi</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">道士</span></span>, "master of the Tao"), thus strictly defining the priests of Taoism, ordained clergymen of a Taoist institution who "represent Taoist culture on a professional basis", are experts of Taoist liturgy, and therefore can employ this knowledge and ritual skills for the benefit of a community.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPregadio2008Vol._1,_p._326,_"Taoshih"_23-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPregadio2008Vol._1,_p._326,_"Taoshih"-23'>[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>This role of Taoist priests reflects the definition of Taoism as a "<a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgical</a> framework for the development of local cults", in other words a scheme or structure for Chinese religion, proposed first by the scholar and Taoist initiate <a href="/wiki/Kristofer_Schipper" title="Kristofer Schipper">Kristofer Schipper</a> in <i>The Taoist Body</i> (1986).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWu2014105–106_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWu2014105–106-24">[24]</a></sup> <i>Daoshi</i> are comparable to the non-Taoist <i>fashi</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">法師</span></span>, "ritual masters") of vernacular traditions (the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Chinese_ritual_mastery_traditions" title="Chinese ritual mastery traditions">Faism</a>") within Chinese religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWu2014105–106_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWu2014105–106-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">dàojiàotú</i></span> (<span lang="zh-Hant">道敎徒</span>; 'follower of Tao'), with the meaning of "Taoist" as "lay member or believer of Taoism", is a modern invention that goes back to the introduction of the Western category of "organized religion" in China in the 20th century, but it has no significance for most of Chinese society in which Taoism continues to be an "order" of the larger body of Chinese religion.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Taoism" title="History of Taoism">History of Taoism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zhang_Lu-Laozi_Riding_an_Ox.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1688" data-file-width="926" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Zhang_Lu-Laozi_Riding_an_Ox.jpg/170px-Zhang_Lu-Laozi_Riding_an_Ox.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Zhang_Lu-Laozi_Riding_an_Ox.jpg/255px-Zhang_Lu-Laozi_Riding_an_Ox.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Zhang_Lu-Laozi_Riding_an_Ox.jpg/340px-Zhang_Lu-Laozi_Riding_an_Ox.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zhang_Lu-Laozi_Riding_an_Ox.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Laozi Riding an Ox</i> (1368–1644) by <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Lu_(painter)" title="Zhang Lu (painter)">Zhang Lu</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a> is traditionally regarded as one of the founders of Taoism and is closely associated in this context with original or primordial Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet199763_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet199763-25">[25]</a></sup> Whether he actually existed is disputed;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet199725_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet199725-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKirkland200462_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKirkland200462-27">[27]</a></sup> however, the work attributed to him—the <i>Tao Te Ching</i>—is dated to the late 4th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKirkland200461_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKirkland200461-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taoism draws its cosmological foundations from the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Naturalists" title="School of Naturalists">School of Naturalists</a> (in the form of its main elements—<a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">yin and yang</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Phases" title="Five Phases">Five Phases</a>), which developed during the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> (4th to 3rd centuries BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinet_1997,_p._6_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robinet_1997,_p._6-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Robinet identifies four components in the emergence of Taoism:
</p>
<ol><li>Philosophical Taoism, i.e. the <i>Tao Te Ching</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi</a></i></li>
<li>techniques for achieving ecstasy</li>
<li>practices for achieving longevity or immortality</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Exorcism" title="Exorcism">exorcism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet199725_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet199725-26">[26]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Some elements of Taoism may be traced to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric" title="Prehistoric">prehistoric</a> folk religions in China that later coalesced into a Taoist tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In particular, many Taoist practices drew from the Warring-States-era phenomena of the <i><a href="/wiki/Wu_(shaman)" title="Wu (shaman)">wu</a></i> (connected to the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_shamanism" title="Chinese shamanism">shamanic culture of northern China</a>) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Fangshi" title="Fangshi">fangshi</a></i> (which probably derived from the "archivist-soothsayers of antiquity, one of whom supposedly was Laozi himself"), even though later Taoists insisted that this was not the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinet_1997,_p._36_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robinet_1997,_p._36-31">[31]</a></sup> Both terms were used to designate individuals dedicated to "... magic, medicine, divination,... methods of longevity and to ecstatic wanderings" as well as exorcism; in the case of the <i>wu</i>, shamans or sorcerers is often used as a translation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinet_1997,_p._36_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robinet_1997,_p._36-31">[31]</a></sup> The <i>fangshi</i> were philosophically close to the School of Naturalists, and relied much on astrological and calendrical speculations in their divinatory activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet199739_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet199739-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wudangshan_pic_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1062" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Wudangshan_pic_7.jpg/220px-Wudangshan_pic_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Wudangshan_pic_7.jpg/330px-Wudangshan_pic_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Wudangshan_pic_7.jpg/440px-Wudangshan_pic_7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wudangshan_pic_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wudang_Mountains" title="Wudang Mountains">Wudangshan</a>, one of the Taoist sacred places</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mawangdui_LaoTsu_Ms2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="453" decoding="async" height="375" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Mawangdui_LaoTsu_Ms2.JPG/170px-Mawangdui_LaoTsu_Ms2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Mawangdui_LaoTsu_Ms2.JPG/255px-Mawangdui_LaoTsu_Ms2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Mawangdui_LaoTsu_Ms2.JPG/340px-Mawangdui_LaoTsu_Ms2.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mawangdui_LaoTsu_Ms2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A part of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist" title="Taoist">Taoist</a> manuscript, ink on silk, 2nd century BCE, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a>, unearthed from <a href="/wiki/Mawangdui" title="Mawangdui">Mawangdui</a> tomb 3rd</div></div></div>
<p>The first organized form of religious Taoism, the <a href="/wiki/Way_of_the_Celestial_Masters" title="Way of the Celestial Masters">Way of the Celestial Masters</a>'s school (later known as Zhengyi school), developed from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Pecks_of_Rice" title="Five Pecks of Rice">Five Pecks of Rice</a> movement at the end of the 2nd century CE; the latter had been founded by Zhang Taoling, who said that Laozi appeared to him in the year 142.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet199754_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet199754-33">[33]</a></sup> The Way of the Celestial Masters school was officially recognized by ruler <a href="/wiki/Cao_Cao" title="Cao Cao">Cao Cao</a> in 215, legitimizing Cao Cao's rise to power in return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet19971_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet19971-34">[34]</a></sup> Laozi received imperial recognition as a divinity in the mid-2nd century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet199750_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet199750-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> (206 BCE–220 CE), the various sources of Taoism had coalesced into a coherent tradition of religious organizations and orders of ritualists in the state of <a href="/wiki/Shu_Han" title="Shu Han">Shu</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>). In earlier ancient China, Taoists were thought of as hermits or recluses who did not participate in political life. Zhuangzi was the best known of these, and it is significant that he lived in the south, where he was part of local <a href="/wiki/Chinese_shamanism" title="Chinese shamanism">Chinese shamanic</a> traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENadeau201242_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENadeau201242-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Female shamans played an important role in this tradition, which was particularly strong in the southern state of <a href="/wiki/Chu_(state)" title="Chu (state)">Chu</a>. Early Taoist movements developed their own institution in contrast to shamanism but absorbed basic shamanic elements. Shamans revealed basic texts of Taoism from early times down to at least the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Institutional orders of Taoism evolved in various strains that in more recent times are conventionally grouped into two main branches: <a href="/wiki/Quanzhen_School" title="Quanzhen School">Quanzhen Taoism</a> and Zhengyi Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChan200593_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChan200593-38">[38]</a></sup> After Laozi and Zhuangzi, the literature of Taoism grew steadily and was compiled in form of a canon—the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tao_Tsang" title="Tao Tsang">Tao Tsang</a>—which was published at the behest of the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">emperor</a>. Throughout Chinese history, Taoism was nominated several times as a <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a>. After the 17th century, it fell from favor.
</p><p>Taoism, in form of the <a href="/wiki/Shangqing_School" title="Shangqing School">Shangqing school</a>, gained official status in China again during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618–907), whose emperors claimed Laozi as their relative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The Shangqing movement had developed much earlier, in the 4th century, on the basis of a series of revelations by gods and spirits to a certain Yang Xi in the years between 364 and 370.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 397 and 402, <a href="/wiki/Ge_Chaofu" title="Ge Chaofu">Ge Chaofu</a> compiled a series of scriptures which later served as the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/Lingbao_School" title="Lingbao School">Lingbao school</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> which unfolded its greatest influence during the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (960–1279).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Several Song emperors, most notably <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Huizong_of_Song" title="Emperor Huizong of Song">Huizong</a>, were active in promoting Taoism, collecting Taoist texts and publishing editions of the <i>Daozang.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guo_Xu_album_dated_1503_(4).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2405" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Guo_Xu_album_dated_1503_%284%29.jpg/220px-Guo_Xu_album_dated_1503_%284%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Guo_Xu_album_dated_1503_%284%29.jpg/330px-Guo_Xu_album_dated_1503_%284%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Guo_Xu_album_dated_1503_%284%29.jpg/440px-Guo_Xu_album_dated_1503_%284%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guo_Xu_album_dated_1503_(4).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Qiu Chuji</i> (1503) by Guo Xu</div></div></div>
<p>In the 12th century, the Quanzhen School was founded in <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a>. It flourished during the 13th and 14th centuries and during the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> became the largest and most important Taoist school in Northern China. The school's most revered master, <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Chuji" title="Qiu Chuji">Qiu Chuji</a>, met with <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> in 1222 and was successful in influencing the Khan towards exerting more restraint during his brutal conquests. By the Khan's decree, the school also was exempt from taxation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEskildsen200417_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEskildsen200417-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aspects of Confucianism, Taoism, and Buddhism were consciously synthesized in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Confucian" title="Neo-Confucian">Neo-Confucian</a> school, which eventually became Imperial <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">orthodoxy</a> for state bureaucratic purposes under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming</a> (1368–1644).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> (1644–1912), however, due to discouragements of the government, many people favored Confucian and Buddhist classics over Taoist works.
</p><p>During the 18th century, the imperial library was constituted, but excluded virtually all Taoist books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> By the beginning of the 20th century, Taoism went through many catastrophic events. (As a result, only one complete copy of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tao_Tsang" title="Tao Tsang">Tao Tsang</a> still remained, at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Cloud_Monastery" title="White Cloud Monastery">White Cloud Monastery</a> in Beijing).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, Taoism is one of five official recognized religions in the People's Republic of China. The government regulates its activities through the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Taoist_Association" title="Chinese Taoist Association">Chinese Taoist Association</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> However, Taoism is practiced without government involvement in <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Taiwan" title="Religion in Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, where it claims millions of adherents.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Sites" title="World Heritage Sites">World Heritage Sites</a> <a href="/wiki/Mount_Qingcheng" title="Mount Qingcheng">Mount Qingcheng</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Longhu" title="Mount Longhu">Mount Longhu</a> are thought to be among the birthplaces of Taoism.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrines">Doctrines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Doctrines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Taoist_philosophy" title="Taoist philosophy">Taoist philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Five_precepts_(Taoism)" title="Five precepts (Taoism)">Five precepts (Taoism)</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Taoism tends to emphasize various themes of the <i>Tao Te Ching</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi</a></i>, such as naturalness, spontaneity, simplicity, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detachment_(philosophy)" title="Detachment (philosophy)">detachment from desires</a>, and most important of all, <a href="/wiki/Wu_wei" title="Wu wei">wu wei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChan1963_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChan1963-49">[49]</a></sup> The concepts of those keystone texts cannot be equated with Taoism as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKirkland20043_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKirkland20043-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tao_and_De">Tao and De</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Tao and De">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">Tao</a> and <a href="/wiki/De_(Chinese)" title="De (Chinese)">De (Chinese)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Weihai-Xianguting-Temple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2248" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Weihai-Xianguting-Temple.jpg/220px-Weihai-Xianguting-Temple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Weihai-Xianguting-Temple.jpg/330px-Weihai-Xianguting-Temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Weihai-Xianguting-Temple.jpg/440px-Weihai-Xianguting-Temple.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Weihai-Xianguting-Temple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Xianguting Temple, a <i><a href="/wiki/Taoist_temple" title="Taoist temple">Taoguan</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Weihai" title="Weihai">Weihai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a>, China</div></div></div>
<p><i>Tao</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">道</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">dào</span></i>) literally means "way", but can also be interpreted as road, channel, path, doctrine, or line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis1996113_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis1996113-51">[51]</a></sup> In Taoism, it is "the One, which is natural, spontaneous, eternal, nameless, and indescribable. It is at once the beginning of all things and the way in which all things pursue their course."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChan1963136_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChan1963136-52">[52]</a></sup> It has variously been denoted as the "flow of the universe",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECane200213_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECane200213-53">[53]</a></sup> a "conceptually necessary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontological" title="Ontological">ontological</a> ground",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> or a demonstration of nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartinson1987168–169_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartinson1987168–169-55">[55]</a></sup> The Tao also is something that individuals can find <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immanent" title="Immanent">immanent</a> in themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFargue1994283_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFargue1994283-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The active expression of Tao is called <i>De</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">德</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">dé</span></i>; also spelled—<i>Te</i> or <i>Teh</i>; often translated with Virtue or Power),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> in a sense that De results from an individual living and cultivating the Tao.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wu-wei">Wu-wei</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wu-wei">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wu_wei" title="Wu wei">Wu wei</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Polysemy" title="Polysemy">polysemous</a> term <i>wu-wei</i> or <i>wuwei</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">無爲</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">wúwéi</span></i>) constitutes the leading ethical concept in Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voorst170_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-voorst170-59">[59]</a></sup> <i>Wei</i> refers to any intentional or deliberated action, while <i>wu</i> carries the meaning of "there is no ..." or "lacking, without". Common translations are nonaction, effortless action, or action without intent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voorst170_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-voorst170-59">[59]</a></sup> The meaning is sometimes emphasized by using the paradoxical expression "wei wu wei": action without action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKirkland200460_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKirkland200460-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In ancient Taoist texts, wu-wei is associated with water through its yielding nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOldmeadow2007109_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOldmeadow2007109-61">[61]</a></sup> Taoist philosophy, in accordance with the <i><a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a></i>, proposes that the universe works harmoniously according to its own ways. When someone exerts their will against the world in a manner that is out of rhythm with the cycles of change, they may disrupt that harmony and <a href="/wiki/Unintended_consequences" title="Unintended consequences">unintended consequences</a> may more likely result rather than the willed outcome. Taoism does not identify one's will as the root problem. Rather, it asserts that one must place their will in harmony with the natural universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaschingdeChant200135_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaschingdeChant200135-62">[62]</a></sup> Thus, a potentially harmful interference may be avoided, and in this way, goals can be achieved effortlessly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChan1963137_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChan1963137-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> "By wu-wei, the sage seeks to come into harmony with the great Tao, which itself accomplishes by nonaction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voorst170_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-voorst170-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ziran">Ziran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ziran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ziran" title="Ziran">Ziran</a></div>
<p><i>Ziran</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">自然</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zìrán</span></i>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">tzu-jan</span></i>; lit. "self-so", "<a href="/wiki/Self-organization" title="Self-organization">self-organization</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zai_2015_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zai_2015-65">[65]</a></sup>) is regarded as a central value in Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2005122_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2005122-66">[66]</a></sup> It describes the "primordial state" of all things<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlingerland200397_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlingerland200397-67">[67]</a></sup> as well as a basic character of the Tao,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGirardot198856_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGirardot198856-68">[68]</a></sup> and is usually associated with spontaneity and creativity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2005121Girardot198856_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2005121Girardot198856-69">[69]</a></sup> To attain naturalness, one has to identify with the Tao;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGirardot198856_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGirardot198856-68">[68]</a></sup> this involves freeing oneself from selfishness and desire, and appreciating simplicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2005122_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2005122-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>An often cited metaphor for naturalness is <i><a href="/wiki/Pu_(Taoism)" title="Pu (Taoism)">pu</a></i> (<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%A8%B8" title="wikt:樸">樸</a></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">pǔ, pú</span></i>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">p'u</span></i>; lit. "uncut wood"), the "uncarved block", which represents the "original nature... prior to the imprint of culture" of an individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKraemer1986286_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKraemer1986286-70">[70]</a></sup> It is usually referred to as a state one returns to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGirardot198870_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGirardot198870-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_Treasures">Three Treasures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Three Treasures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Three_Treasures_(Taoism)" title="Three Treasures (Taoism)">Three Treasures (Taoism)</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Three_Treasures_(traditional_Chinese_medicine)" title="Three Treasures (traditional Chinese medicine)">Three Treasures (traditional Chinese medicine)</a></div>
<p>The Taoist <i>Three Treasures</i> or <i>Three Jewels</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">三寶</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">sānbǎo</span></i>) comprise the basic virtues of <i>ci</i> (<span lang="zh">慈</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">cí</span></i>, usually translated as <i>compassion</i>), <i>jian</i> (<span lang="zh">儉</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">jiǎn</span></i>, usually translated as <i>moderation</i>), and <i>bugan wei tianxia xian</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">不敢爲天下先</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">bùgǎn wéi tiānxià xiān</span></i>, literally "not daring to act as first under the heavens", but usually translated as <i>humility</i>).
</p><p>As the practical, political side of Taoist philosophy, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Waley" title="Arthur Waley">Arthur Waley</a> translated them as "abstention from aggressive war and capital punishment", "absolute simplicity of living", and "refusal to assert active authority".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Three Treasures can also refer to <a href="/wiki/Jing_(Chinese_medicine)" title="Jing (Chinese medicine)">jing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine#Qi" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">qi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shen_(Chinese_religion)" title="Shen (Chinese religion)">shen</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">精氣神</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">jīng-qì-shén</span></i>; jing is usually translated as <i>essence,</i> qi as <i>life force</i>, and shen as <i>spirit</i>). These terms are elements of the <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine#Model_of_the_body" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese concept of the human body</a>, which shares its cosmological foundation—<a href="/wiki/School_of_Naturalists" title="School of Naturalists">Yinyangism</a> or the Naturalists—with Taoism. Within this framework, they play an important role in <a href="/wiki/Neidan" title="Neidan">neidan</a> ("Taoist Inner Alchemy").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmology">Cosmology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cosmology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/School_of_Naturalists" title="School of Naturalists">School of Naturalists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">Qi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taoism_and_death" title="Taoism and death">Taoism and death</a></div>
<p>Taoist cosmology is <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_model" title="Cyclic model">cyclic</a>—the universe is seen as being in a constant process of re-creating itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvp|Robinet|1997|p=7_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-harvp|Robinet|1997|p=7-74">[74]</a></sup>  Evolution and 'extremes meet' are main characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zai_2015_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zai_2015-65">[65]</a></sup> Taoist cosmology shares similar views with the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Naturalists" title="School of Naturalists">School of Naturalists</a> (Yinyang)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinet_1997,_p._6_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robinet_1997,_p._6-29">[29]</a></sup> which was headed by <a href="/wiki/Zou_Yan" title="Zou Yan">Zou Yan</a> (305–240 BCE). The school's tenets harmonized the concepts of the <a href="/wiki/Wuxing_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Wuxing (Chinese philosophy)">Wu Xing</a> (Five Elements) and <i><a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">yin and yang</a></i>. In this spirit, the universe is seen as being in a constant process of re-creating itself, as everything that exists is a mere aspect of <i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i>, which "condensed, becomes life; diluted, it is indefinite potential".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvp|Robinet|1997|p=7_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-harvp|Robinet|1997|p=7-74">[74]</a></sup> Qi is in a perpetual transformation between its condensed and diluted state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinet_1997,_p._8_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robinet_1997,_p._8-75">[75]</a></sup> These two different states of qi, on the other hand, are embodiments of the abstract entities of yin and yang,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinet_1997,_p._8_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robinet_1997,_p._8-75">[75]</a></sup> two complementary extremes that constantly play against and with each other and one cannot exist without the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet19979_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet19979-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Human beings are seen as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrocosm_and_microcosm" title="Macrocosm and microcosm">microcosm</a> of the universe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinet_1997,_p._103_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robinet_1997,_p._103-20">[20]</a></sup> and for example comprise the Wu Xing in form of the <a href="/wiki/Zang-fu" title="Zang-fu">zang-fu</a> organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> As a consequence, it is believed that a deeper understanding of the universe can be achieved by understanding oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOcchiogrosso1994171_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOcchiogrosso1994171-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist_theology" title="Taoist theology">Taoist theology</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">Xian (Taoism)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Li_Hong_(Taoist_eschatology)" title="Li Hong (Taoist eschatology)">Li Hong (Taoist eschatology)</a></div>
<p>Taoist theology can be defined as <a href="/wiki/Apophatic_theology" title="Apophatic theology">apophatic</a>, given its philosophical emphasis on the formlessness and unknowable nature of the Tao, and the primacy of the "Way" rather than anthropomorphic concepts of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>. This is one of the core beliefs that nearly all the sects share.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet19971_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet19971-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taoist orders usually present the <a href="/wiki/Three_Pure_Ones" title="Three Pure Ones">Three Pure Ones</a> at the top of the pantheon of deities, visualizing the hierarchy emanating from the Tao. <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a> is considered the incarnation of one of the Three Purities and worshiped as the ancestor of the philosophical doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinet199763_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinet199763-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Different branches of Taoism often have differing pantheons of lesser deities, where these deities reflect different notions of cosmology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Lesser deities also may be promoted or demoted for their activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Some varieties of popular Chinese religion incorporate the <a href="/wiki/Jade_Emperor" title="Jade Emperor">Jade Emperor</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Y%C3%BC-Huang&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Yü-Huang (page does not exist)">Yü-Huang</a>) or (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Y%C3%BC-Di&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Yü-Di (page does not exist)">Yü-Di</a>)), derived from the main of the Three Purities, as a representation of the most high God.
</p><p>Persons from the history of Taoism, and people who are considered to have become immortals (<i><a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">xian</a></i>), are venerated as well by both clergy and laypeople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite these hierarchies of deities, traditional conceptions of Tao should not be confused with the Western <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theism</a>. Being one with the Tao does not necessarily indicate a union with an eternal spirit in, for example, the Hindu sense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartinson1987168–169_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartinson1987168–169-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaschingdeChant200135_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaschingdeChant200135-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Texts">Texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tao_Te_Ching"><i>Tao Te Ching</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Tao Te Ching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tao_Te_Ching" title="Tao Te Ching">Tao Te Ching</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DaoDeJingWangBi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="408" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/DaoDeJingWangBi.jpg/170px-DaoDeJingWangBi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/DaoDeJingWangBi.jpg/255px-DaoDeJingWangBi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/DaoDeJingWangBi.jpg/340px-DaoDeJingWangBi.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DaoDeJingWangBi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1770 <i>Wang Bi</i> edition of the <i>Tao Te Ching</i></div></div></div>
<p>The <i>Tao Te Ching</i> or <i>Daodejing</i> is widely considered the most influential Taoist text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> According to legend, it was written by <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> and often the book is simply referred to as the <i>Laozi</i>. Authorship, precise date of origin, and even unity of the text are still subject of debate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliade198426_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliade198426-85">[85]</a></sup> and will probably never be known with certainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatts1975xxiii_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatts1975xxiii-86">[86]</a></sup> The earliest texts of the <i>Tao Te Ching</i> that have been excavated (written on <a href="/wiki/Guodian_Chu_Slips" title="Guodian Chu Slips">bamboo tablets</a>) date back to the late 4th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanford_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-87">[87]</a></sup> Throughout the history of religious Taoism, the <i>Tao Te Ching</i> has been used as a ritual text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohnLaFargue1998158_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohnLaFargue1998158-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The opening lines of the <i>Tao Te Ching</i> are:
</p>
<blockquote><div class="poem">
<p><span lang="zh-Hant">道可道非常道</span> (<a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">dào kě dào fēi cháng dào</span></i>)<br/>
The Tao that can be told is not the eternal Tao<br/>
<br/>
<span lang="zh-Hant">名可名非常名</span> (<a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">míng kě míng fēi cháng míng</span></i>)<br/>
The name that can be named is not the eternal name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lao_Tzu_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lao_Tzu-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<p>There is significant, at times acrimonious, debate regarding which English translation of the Tao Te Ching is preferable, and which particular translation methodology is best.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The <i>Tao Te Ching</i> is not thematically ordered. The main themes of the text are repeatedly expressed using variant formulations, often with only a slight difference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim200313_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim200313-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The leading themes revolve around the nature of Tao and how to attain it. Tao is said to be ineffable and accomplishes great things through small means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Voorst2005165_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Voorst2005165-92">[92]</a></sup> Ancient commentaries on the <i>Tao Te Ching</i> are important texts in their own right. Perhaps the oldest one, the <i><a href="/wiki/Heshang_Gong" title="Heshang Gong">Heshang Gong</a></i> commentary, was most likely written in the 2nd century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Other important commentaries include the one from <a href="/wiki/Wang_Bi" title="Wang Bi">Wang Bi</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Xiang%27er" title="Xiang'er">Xiang'er</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zhuangzi"><i>Zhuangzi</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Zhuangzi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi (book)</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)"><i>Zhuangzi</i></a> or <i>Chuang Tzu</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">莊子</span>), named after its traditional author <a href="/wiki/Zhuang_Zhou" title="Zhuang Zhou">Zhuangzi</a>, is a composite of writings from various sources, and is generally considered the most important of all Taoist writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIdemaHaft199790_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIdemaHaft199790-95">[95]</a></sup> The commentator <a href="/wiki/Guo_Xiang" title="Guo Xiang">Guo Xiang</a> (c. CE 300) helped establish the text as an important source for Taoist thought. The traditional view is that Zhuangzi himself wrote the first seven chapters (the "inner chapters") and his students and related thinkers were responsible for the other parts (the outer and miscellaneous chapters). The work uses anecdotes, parables and dialogues to express one of its main themes, that is aligning oneself to the laws of the natural world and "the way" of the elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="I_Ching"><i>I Ching</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: I Ching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pakua.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="430" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Pakua.svg/170px-Pakua.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Pakua.svg/255px-Pakua.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Pakua.svg/340px-Pakua.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pakua.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The eight trigrams of the <i>I Ching</i>, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Bagua" title="Bagua">bagua</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a></i> or <i>Yijing</i> was originally a divination system that had its origins around 1150 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Although it predates the first mentions of Tao as an organized system of philosophy and religious practice, this text later became of philosophical importance to Taoism and Confucianism.
</p><p>The <i>I Ching</i> itself, shorn of its commentaries, consists of 64 combinations of 8 trigrams (called "hexagrams"), traditionally chosen by throwing coins or yarrow sticks, to give the diviner some idea of the situation at hand and, through reading of the "changing lines", some idea of what is developing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 64 original notations of the hexagrams in the <i>I<span class="nowrap"> </span>Ching</i> can also be read as a meditation on how change occurs, so it assists Taoists with managing <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">yin and yang</a> cycles as Laozi advocated in the <i>Tao Te Ching</i> (the oldest known version of this text was dated to 400 BCE). More recently as recorded in the 18th century, the Taoist master <a href="/wiki/Liu_Yiming" title="Liu Yiming">Liu Yiming</a> continued to advocate this usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClearyTIC_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClearyTIC-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Taoist_Canon"><i>The Taoist Canon</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: The Taoist Canon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Daozang" title="Daozang">Daozang</a></div>
<p>The Taoist Canon (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">道藏</span></span>, <i>Treasury of Tao</i>) is also referred to as the <i>Daozang</i>. It was originally compiled during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin_Dynasty_(265-420)" title="Jin Dynasty (265-420)">Jin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a> dynasties. The extant version was published during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The Ming <i>Daozang</i> includes almost 1500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred_text" title="Sacred text">texts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Following the example of the Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Tripi%E1%B9%ADaka" title="Tripiṭaka">Tripiṭaka</a>, it is divided into three <i>dong</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">洞</span></span>, "caves", "grottoes"). They are arranged from "highest" to "lowest":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">Zhen</a></i> ("real" or "truth" <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">眞</span></span>) grotto. Includes the <a href="/wiki/Shangqing_School" title="Shangqing School">Shangqing</a> texts.</li>
<li>The <i>Xuan</i> ("mystery" <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">玄</span></span>) grotto. Includes the <a href="/wiki/Lingbao_School" title="Lingbao School">Lingbao</a> scriptures.</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Shen_(Chinese_religion)" title="Shen (Chinese religion)">Shen</a></i> ("divine" <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">神</span></span>) grotto. Includes texts predating the Maoshan (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">茅山</span></span>) revelations.</li></ol>
<p><i>Taoist</i> generally do not consult published versions of the Daozang, but individually choose, or inherit, texts included in the Daozang. These texts have been passed down for generations from teacher to student.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Shangqing_School" title="Shangqing School">Shangqing School</a> has a tradition of approaching Taoism through scriptural study. It is believed that by reciting certain texts often enough one will be rewarded with immortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_texts">Other texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Other texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While the Tao Te Ching is most well-known, there are many other important texts in traditional Taoism. <i><a href="/wiki/Treatise_On_the_Response_of_the_Tao" title="Treatise On the Response of the Tao">Taishang Ganying Pian</a></i> ("Treatise of the Exalted One on Response and Retribution") discusses sin and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethic" title="Ethic">ethics</a>, and has become a popular morality tract in the last few centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> It asserts that those in harmony with Tao will live long and fruitful lives. The wicked, and their descendants, will suffer and have shortened lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Voorst2005165_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Voorst2005165-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols_and_images">Symbols and images</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Symbols and images">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yin_yang.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Yin_yang.svg/170px-Yin_yang.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Yin_yang.svg/255px-Yin_yang.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Yin_yang.svg/340px-Yin_yang.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yin_yang.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Basic depiction of the <i><a href="/wiki/Taijitu" title="Taijitu">taijitu</a></i> symbol without trigrams</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E6%96%87%E5%85%89%E5%A1%94-%E4%BA%8C%E5%B1%82%E8%97%BB%E4%BA%95.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/%E6%96%87%E5%85%89%E5%A1%94-%E4%BA%8C%E5%B1%82%E8%97%BB%E4%BA%95.JPG/220px-%E6%96%87%E5%85%89%E5%A1%94-%E4%BA%8C%E5%B1%82%E8%97%BB%E4%BA%95.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/%E6%96%87%E5%85%89%E5%A1%94-%E4%BA%8C%E5%B1%82%E8%97%BB%E4%BA%95.JPG/330px-%E6%96%87%E5%85%89%E5%A1%94-%E4%BA%8C%E5%B1%82%E8%97%BB%E4%BA%95.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/%E6%96%87%E5%85%89%E5%A1%94-%E4%BA%8C%E5%B1%82%E8%97%BB%E4%BA%95.JPG/440px-%E6%96%87%E5%85%89%E5%A1%94-%E4%BA%8C%E5%B1%82%E8%97%BB%E4%BA%95.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E6%96%87%E5%85%89%E5%A1%94-%E4%BA%8C%E5%B1%82%E8%97%BB%E4%BA%95.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Caisson_(Asian_architecture)" title="Caisson (Asian architecture)"><i>spider web ceiling</i></a> depicting a <i><a href="/wiki/Taijitu" title="Taijitu">taijitu</a></i> surrounded by the <i><a href="/wiki/Bagua" title="Bagua">Bagua</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Taijitu" title="Taijitu">taijitu</a></i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">太極圖</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">tàijítú</span></i>; commonly known as the "yin and yang symbol" or simply the "yin yang") and the <i><a href="/wiki/Bagua" title="Bagua">Bagua</a></i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">八卦</span></span> ("Eight Trigrams") have importance in Taoist symbolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> In this cosmology, the universe creates itself out of a primary chaos of material energy, organized into the cycles of Yin and Yang and formed into objects and lives. Yin is the receptive and Yang is the active principle, seen in all forms of change and difference such as the annual season cycles, the natural landscape, the formation of both men and women as characters, and sociopolitical history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> While almost all Taoist organizations make use of it, its principles have influenced Confucian, Neo-Confucian or pan-Chinese theory. One can see this symbol as a decorative element on Taoist organization flags and logos, temple floors, or stitched into clerical robes. According to Song dynasty sources, it originated around the 10th century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Little_2000,_p._131_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Little_2000,_p._131-109">[109]</a></sup> Previously, a tiger and a dragon had symbolized yin and yang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Little_2000,_p._131_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Little_2000,_p._131-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taoist temples may fly square or triangular flags.
