They typically feature mystical writing or diagrams and are intended to fulfill various functions including providing guidance for the spirits of the dead, bringing good fortune, increasing life span, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Other flags and banners may be those of the gods or immortals themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>A zigzag with seven stars is sometimes displayed, representing the <a href="/wiki/Big_Dipper" title="Big Dipper">Big Dipper</a> (or the Bushel, the Chinese equivalent). In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shang_Dynasty" title="Shang Dynasty">Shang Dynasty</a> of the 2nd millennium BCE, Chinese thought regarded the Big Dipper as a deity, while during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a>, it was considered a <i>qi</i> path of the circumpolar god, <i>Taiyi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleEichman2000128_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleEichman2000128-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taoist temples in southern China and Taiwan may often be identified by their roofs, which feature <a href="/wiki/Chinese_dragon" title="Chinese dragon">dragons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fenghuang" title="Fenghuang">phoenixes</a> made from multicolored ceramic tiles. They also stand for the harmony of yin and yang (with the phoenix representing yin). A related symbol is the flaming pearl, which may be seen on such roofs between two dragons, as well as on the hairpin of a <a href="/wiki/List_of_Celestial_Masters" title="List of Celestial Masters">Celestial Master</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchipper199321_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchipper199321-113">[113]</a></sup> In general though, Chinese Taoist architecture lacks universal features that distinguish it from other structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleEichman200074_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleEichman200074-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practices">Practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rituals">Rituals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Rituals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E4%BA%8C%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%99%E5%86%85%E6%AE%BF.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2482" data-file-width="3707" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%E4%BA%8C%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%99%E5%86%85%E6%AE%BF.jpg/220px-%E4%BA%8C%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%99%E5%86%85%E6%AE%BF.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%E4%BA%8C%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%99%E5%86%85%E6%AE%BF.jpg/330px-%E4%BA%8C%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%99%E5%86%85%E6%AE%BF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%E4%BA%8C%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%99%E5%86%85%E6%AE%BF.jpg/440px-%E4%BA%8C%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%99%E5%86%85%E6%AE%BF.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E4%BA%8C%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%99%E5%86%85%E6%AE%BF.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A hall of worship of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erwang_Temple" title="Erwang Temple">Erwang Temple</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Taoist_temple" title="Taoist temple">Taoist temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dujiangyan_City" title="Dujiangyan City">Dujiangyan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>. There are elements of the <i><a href="/wiki/Jingxiang" title="Jingxiang">jingxiang</a></i> religious practice (<a href="/wiki/Incense" title="Incense">incense</a> and <a href="/wiki/Candle" title="Candle">candle</a> offerings).</div></div></div>
<p>In ancient times, before the Taoism religion was founded, food would sometimes be set out as a <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifice</a> to the spirits of the deceased or the gods. This could include slaughtered animals, such as pigs and ducks, or fruit. The Taoist <a href="/wiki/List_of_Celestial_Masters" title="List of Celestial Masters">Celestial Master</a> <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Daoling" title="Zhang Daoling">Zhang Daoling</a> rejected food and <a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice" title="Animal sacrifice">animal sacrifices</a> to the Gods. He tore apart temples, which demanded animal sacrifice and drove away its priests. This rejection of sacrifices has continued into the modern day, as Taoism Temples are not allowed to use animal sacrifices (with the exception of folk temples or local tradition.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Another form of sacrifice involves the burning of <a href="/wiki/Joss_paper" title="Joss paper">joss paper</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Hell_money" title="Hell money">hell money</a>, on the assumption that images thus consumed by the fire will reappear—not as a mere image, but as the actual item—in the spirit world, making them available for revered ancestors and departed loved ones. The joss paper is mostly used when memorializing ancestors, such as done during the <a href="/wiki/Qingming_Festival" title="Qingming Festival">Qingming</a> festival.
</p><p>Also on particular holidays, street parades take place. These are lively affairs that involve firecrackers and flower-covered floats broadcasting traditional music. They also variously include <a href="/wiki/Lion_dance" title="Lion dance">lion dances</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dragon_dance" title="Dragon dance">dragon dances</a>; human-occupied puppets (often of the <a href="/wiki/Heibai_Wuchang" title="Heibai Wuchang">"Seventh Lord" and "Eighth Lord"</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kungfu" title="Kungfu">Kungfu</a>-practicing and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palanquin" title="Palanquin">palanquins</a> carrying god-images. The various participants are not considered performers, but rather possessed by the gods and spirits in question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fortune-telling" title="Fortune-telling">Fortune-telling</a>—including <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrology</a>, <a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a>, and other forms of <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a>—has long been considered a traditional Taoist pursuit. <a href="/wiki/Mediumship" title="Mediumship">Mediumship</a> is also widely encountered in some sects. There is an academic and social distinction between martial forms of mediumship (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tongji_(spirit_medium)" title="Tongji (spirit medium)">tongji</a></i>) and the spirit-writing that is typically practiced through <a href="/wiki/Fuji_(planchette_writing)" title="Fuji (planchette writing)">planchette writing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_cultivation">Physical cultivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Physical cultivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neidan" title="Neidan">Neidan</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist_alchemy" title="Taoist alchemy">Taoist alchemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neijia" title="Neijia">Neijia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_woodcut;_Alchemical_refining_furnace_Wellcome_L0038814.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3104" data-file-width="2069" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Chinese_woodcut%3B_Alchemical_refining_furnace_Wellcome_L0038814.jpg/170px-Chinese_woodcut%3B_Alchemical_refining_furnace_Wellcome_L0038814.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Chinese_woodcut%3B_Alchemical_refining_furnace_Wellcome_L0038814.jpg/255px-Chinese_woodcut%3B_Alchemical_refining_furnace_Wellcome_L0038814.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Chinese_woodcut%3B_Alchemical_refining_furnace_Wellcome_L0038814.jpg/340px-Chinese_woodcut%3B_Alchemical_refining_furnace_Wellcome_L0038814.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_woodcut;_Alchemical_refining_furnace_Wellcome_L0038814.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese woodblock illustration of a <i>waidan</i> alchemical refining furnace, 1856 <i>Illustrated Manual of External Medicine</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">外科圖說</span></span>)</div></div></div>
<p>A recurrent and important element of Taoism are rituals, exercises and substances aiming at aligning oneself spiritually with cosmic forces, at undertaking ecstatic spiritual journeys, or at improving physical health and thereby extending one's life, ideally to the point of immortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Enlightened and immortal beings are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">xian</a>.
</p><p>A characteristic method aiming for longevity is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist_alchemy" title="Taoist alchemy">Taoist alchemy</a>. Already in very early Taoist scriptures—like the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taiping_Jing" title="Taiping Jing">Taiping Jing</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Baopuzi" title="Baopuzi">Baopuzi</a></i>—alchemical formulas for achieving immortality were outlined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of martial arts traditions, particularly the ones falling under the category of <a href="/wiki/Neijia" title="Neijia">Neijia</a> (like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T%27ai_chi_ch%27uan" title="T'ai chi ch'uan">T'ai Chi Ch'uan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pa_Kwa_Chang" title="Pa Kwa Chang">Pa Kwa Chang</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xingyiquan" title="Xingyiquan">Xing Yi Quan</a>) embody Taoist principles to a significant extent, and some practitioners consider their art a means of practicing Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adherents">Adherents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Adherents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WhiteCloudTemplepic9.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/WhiteCloudTemplepic9.jpg/220px-WhiteCloudTemplepic9.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/WhiteCloudTemplepic9.jpg/330px-WhiteCloudTemplepic9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/WhiteCloudTemplepic9.jpg/440px-WhiteCloudTemplepic9.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WhiteCloudTemplepic9.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/White_Cloud_Temple" title="White Cloud Temple">White Cloud Temple</a> in Beijing</div></div></div>
<p>The number of Taoists is difficult to estimate, due to a variety of factors including defining Taoism. According to a survey of <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_China" title="Religion in China">religion in China</a> in the year 2010, the number of people practicing some form of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">Chinese folk religion</a> is near to 950 million (70% of the Chinese).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSLS2010_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSLS2010-121">[121]</a></sup> Among these, 173 million (13%) claim an affiliation with Taoist practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSLS2010_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-CSLS2010-121">[121]</a></sup> Furthermore, 12 million people stated that they were "Taoists", a term traditionally used exclusively for initiates, priests and experts of Taoist rituals and methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSLS2010_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-CSLS2010-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Chinese people and many others have been influenced in some way by Taoist traditions. Since the creation of the People's Republic of China, the government has encouraged a revival of Taoist traditions in codified settings. In 1956, the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Taoist_Association" title="Chinese Taoist Association">Chinese Taoist Association</a> was formed to administer the activities of all registered Taoist orders, and received official approval in 1957. It was disbanded during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a> under <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a>, but was reestablished in 1980. The headquarters of the association are at the <i>Baiyunguan</i>, or <a href="/wiki/White_Cloud_Temple" title="White Cloud Temple">White Cloud Temple</a> of <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, belonging to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Longmen_Taoism" title="Longmen Taoism">Longmen</a> branch of Quanzhen Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Since 1980, many Taoist monasteries and temples have been reopened or rebuilt, both belonging to the Zhengyi or Quanzhen schools, and clergy <a href="/wiki/Ordination" title="Ordination">ordination</a> has been resumed.
</p><p>Taoist literature and art has influenced the cultures of <a href="/wiki/Taoism_in_Korea" title="Taoism in Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism_in_Japan" title="Taoism in Japan">Japan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taoism_in_Vietnam" title="Taoism in Vietnam">Vietnam</a>. Organized Taoism seems not to have attracted a large non-Chinese following until modern times. In <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, 7.5 million people (33% of the population) identify themselves as Taoists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moi_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-moi-123">[123]</a></sup> Data collected in 2010 for religious demographics of <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> show that, respectively, 14% and 11% of the people of these cities identify as Taoists.
</p><p>Followers of Taoism are also present in Chinese émigré communities outside Asia. In addition, it has attracted followers with no Chinese heritage. For example, in Brazil there are Taoist temples in São Paulo and Rio de Janeiro which are affiliated with the Taoist Society of China.  Membership of these temples is entirely of non-Chinese ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_poetry">Art and poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Art and poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Taoist_art" title="Taoist art">Taoist art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%85%AD%E6%9F%BF%E5%9B%BE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="491" decoding="async" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%E5%85%AD%E6%9F%BF%E5%9B%BE.jpg/220px-%E5%85%AD%E6%9F%BF%E5%9B%BE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%E5%85%AD%E6%9F%BF%E5%9B%BE.jpg/330px-%E5%85%AD%E6%9F%BF%E5%9B%BE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%E5%85%AD%E6%9F%BF%E5%9B%BE.jpg/440px-%E5%85%AD%E6%9F%BF%E5%9B%BE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%85%AD%E6%9F%BF%E5%9B%BE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Six_Persimmons" title="Six Persimmons">Six Persimmons</a></i>, a Taoist-influenced 13th-century Chinese painting by the monk <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu_Qi" title="Mu Qi">Mu Qi</a></div></div></div>
<p>Throughout Chinese history, there have been many examples of art being influenced by Taoist thought. Notable painters influenced by Taoism include <a href="/wiki/Wu_Wei_(painter)" title="Wu Wei (painter)">Wu Wei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huang_Gongwang" title="Huang Gongwang">Huang Gongwang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mi_Fu" title="Mi Fu">Mi Fu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muqi_Fachang" title="Muqi Fachang">Muqi Fachang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shitao" title="Shitao">Shitao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ni_Zan" title="Ni Zan">Ni Zan</a>, Tang Mi, and Wang Zengzu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang1968_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang1968-127">[127]</a></sup> Taoist arts represents the diverse regions, dialects, and time spans that are commonly associated with Taoism. Ancient Taoist art was commissioned by the aristocracy; however, scholars masters and adepts also directly engaged in the art themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_aspects">Political aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Political aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Taoism never had a unified political theory. While Huang-Lao's positions justified a strong emperor as the legitimate ruler,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2000224–226,_370–374_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2000224–226,_370–374-129">[129]</a></sup> the "primitivists" (like in the chapters 8-11 of the Zhuangzi) argued strongly for a radical <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchism</a>. A more moderate position is presented in the Inner Chapters of the <a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi</a> in which the political life is presented with disdain and some kind of pluralism or perspectivism is preferred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraham1989172,_306–311_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraham1989172,_306–311-130">[130]</a></sup> The syncretist position in texts like the Huainanzi and some Outer Chapters of the Zhuangzi blended some Taoist positions with Confucian ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_other_religions_and_philosophies">Relations with other religions and philosophies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taoism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Relations with other religions and philosophies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Three_teachings" title="Three teachings">Three teachings</a></div>
<p>Many scholars believe Taoism arose as a countermovement to <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher1997167_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher1997167-132">[132]</a></sup> The philosophical terms <i>Tao</i> and <i>De</i> are indeed shared by both Taoism and Confucianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkhamRuparell2001254_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkhamRuparell2001254-133">[133]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zhuang_Zhou" title="Zhuang Zhou">Zhuangzi</a> explicitly criticized Confucian and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohist" title="Mohist">Mohist</a> tenets in his work. In general, Taoism rejects the Confucian emphasis on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_(Confucian)" title="Li (Confucian)">rituals</a>, hierarchical social order, and conventional morality, and favors "naturalness", spontaneity, and individualism instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaspero198139_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaspero198139-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>The entry of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">Buddhism</a> into China was marked by significant interaction and <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a> with Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaspero198146_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaspero198146-135">[135]</a></sup> Originally seen as a kind of "foreign Taoism", Buddhism's scriptures were translated into Chinese using the Taoist vocabulary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrebish1975192_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrebish1975192-136">[136]</a></sup> Representatives of early Chinese Buddhism, like <a href="/wiki/Sengzhao" title="Sengzhao">Sengzhao</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tao_Sheng" title="Tao Sheng">Tao Sheng</a>, knew and were deeply influenced by the Taoist keystone texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDumoulinHeisigKnitter200570,_74_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDumoulinHeisigKnitter200570,_74-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taoism especially shaped the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Chan" title="Chinese Chan">Chan (Zen)</a> Buddhism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMollier2008_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMollier2008-138">[138]</a></sup> introducing elements like the concept of <i>naturalness</i>, distrust of scripture and text, and emphasis on embracing "this life" and living in the "every-moment".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDumoulinHeisigKnitter200568,_70–73,_167–168_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDumoulinHeisigKnitter200568,_70–73,_167–168-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, Taoism also incorporated Buddhist elements during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>. Examples of such influence include monasteries, vegetarianism, prohibition of alcohol, the doctrine of emptiness, and collecting scripture in tripartite organization in certain sects.
</p><p>Ideological and political rivals for centuries, Taoism, Confucianism, and Buddhism deeply influenced one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkhamRuparell2001248–249_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkhamRuparell2001248–249-140">[140]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Wang_Bi" title="Wang Bi">Wang Bi</a>, one of the most influential philosophical commentators on Laozi (and the <i><a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a></i>), was a Confucian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchipper1993192_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchipper1993192-141">[141]</a></sup> The three rivals also share some similar values, with all three embracing a <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanist</a> philosophy emphasizing moral behavior and human perfection. In time, most Chinese people identified to some extent with all three traditions simultaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-window_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-window-142">[142]</a></sup> This became institutionalized when aspects of the three schools were synthesized in the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucian</a> school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore1967133,_147_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore1967133,_147-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Comparisons between Taoism and <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureanism</a> have focused on the absence of a creator or gods controlling the forces of nature in both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Lucretius' poem <a href="/wiki/De_rerum_natura" title="De rerum natura">De rerum natura</a> describes a naturalist cosmology where there are only atoms and void (a primal duality which mirrors Ying/Yang in its dance of assertion/yielding), and where nature takes its course with no gods or masters. Other parallels include the similarities between Taoist "wu wei" (effortless action) and Epicurean "lathe biosas" (live unknown), focus on naturalness (ziran) as opposed to conventional virtues, and the prominence of the Epicurus-like Chinese sage Yang Chu in the foundational Taoist writings.
</p><p>Some authors have undertaken <a href="/wiki/Comparative_religion" title="Comparative religion">comparative studies</a> of Taoism and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. This has been of interest for students of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_religion" title="History of religion">history of religion</a> such as <a href="/wiki/J._J._M._de_Groot" title="J. J. M. de Groot">J. J. M. de Groot</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWerblowsky200225_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWerblowsky200225-145">[145]</a></sup> among others. A comparison of the teachings of Laozi and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_of_Nazareth" title="Jesus of Nazareth">Jesus of Nazareth</a> has been made by several authors, such as Martin Aronson,<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAronson2002[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_March_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(March_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_146-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAronson2002[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_March_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(March_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-146'>[146]</a></sup> and Toropov &amp; Hansen (2002), who believe that there are parallels that should not be ignored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToropovHansen2002169–181_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToropovHansen2002169–181-147">[147]</a></sup> In the opinion of J. Isamu Yamamoto, the main difference is that Christianity preaches a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personal_God" title="Personal God">personal God</a> while Taoism does not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYamamoto199869–70_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYamamoto199869–70-148">[148]</a></sup> Yet, a number of authors, including <a href="/wiki/Lin_Yutang" title="Lin Yutang">Lin Yutang</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERuokanenZhanzhu_Huang2010137_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERuokanenZhanzhu_Huang2010137-149">[149]</a></sup> have argued that some moral and ethical tenets of the religions are similar.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhiming2010[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_March_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(March_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_150-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhiming2010[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_March_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(March_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-150'>[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChung2001141–145_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChung2001141–145-151">[151]</a></sup> In neighboring <a href="/wiki/Taoism_in_Vietnam" title="Taoism in Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, Taoist values have been shown to adapt to social norms and formed emerging sociocultural beliefs together with Confucianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENapierPhamNguyenNguyen2018_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENapierPhamNguyenNguyen2018-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-stanford-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-stanford_87-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/laozi/" rel="nofollow">"Laozi"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>. 2018. <q>The discovery of two <i>Laozi</i> silk manuscripts at Mawangdui, near Changsha, Hunan province in 1973 marks an important milestone in modern <i>Laozi</i> research. The manuscripts, identified simply as "A" (jia) and "B" (yi), were found in a tomb that was sealed in 168 B.C. The texts themselves can be dated earlier, the "A" manuscript being the older of the two, copied in all likelihood before 195 B.C.<br/>Until recently, the Mawangdui manuscripts have held the pride of place as the oldest extant manuscripts of the <i>Laozi</i>. In late 1993, the excavation of a tomb (identified as M1) in Guodian, Jingmen city, Hubei province, has yielded among other things some 800 bamboo slips, of which 730 are inscribed, containing over 13,000 Chinese characters. Some of these, amounting to about 2,000 characters, match the <i>Laozi</i>. The tomb...is dated around 300 B.C.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Laozi&amp;rft.btitle=Stanford+Encyclopedia+of+Philosophy&amp;rft.pub=Stanford+University&amp;rft.date=2018&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fplato.stanford.edu%2Fentries%2Flaozi%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATaoism"></span></span>
</li>
